RATING: 7.5
To start with this game has an AMAZING table presence. Between the theme and the bits the game starts selling itself before you even start playing. Laying out the tiles to create the garden, growing the bamboo, moving the panda and the gardener, and putting out the irrigation are all steps that are engaging and that people grasp really quickly in my experience.

I really enjoy this light family game. It is fast-paced and doesn't cause too much AP in players. In addition, the game introduces the concept of hidden objectives which is something a lot of non-gamers aren't familiar with outside of those who remember Careers so it is a nice building block to future games. 

One negative is that it can feel somewhat unbalanced with regards to the objective cards. The Plot objectives do not seem to reward on par with the gardener cards for the amount of effort expended. Knowing that the gardener cards have higher scores is an advantage. I think the variant for objective cards could help fix the "lucky draw" problem.

RATING: 7
As part of the Imperial court, players first roll dice to determine the weather which gives them a bonus (take another action or an improvement tile). Players can choose to expand the garden, irrigate a plot, move the gardener, move the panda or draw a bonus card.  Players are trying to score points through their secret bonus cards by fulfilling the conditions on the card. Bonus cards are matching the colored plots, having the panda consume the right colors or having certain configurations of bamboo.

There is luck in what you roll and the bonus cards you draw. It's fun, and more in the filler category. I enjoy playing it and the components are nice. There is a racing element and there are tough decisions to make.  Looking forward to more plays.

RATING: 8.4
This game not only looks gorgeous and easy to set up and taken down but it's also easy to teach, easy to learn and a joy to play. It plays like a light, well made Euro game but with the stronger components and more lighthearted look and feel, plus the addition of the die, it doesn't suffer from the dryness that many euro games of this weight have. In terms of constant replayability--it's debatable if this is something you'd want to play each and every week (though I don't think it has bad replayability as there are clearly layers of depth and strategy)

This game is a real winner and even the inlay works really well--almost as well as Jamaica. Having the 3d stacking bamboo and painted minis is great and the choice of a bright and happy aesthetic is perfect. I use the mists of Pandaria soundtrack while playing this game. 

I recommend it for people who like light and family friendly games 1-4, easy/introductory Euro games, or games that are easy to play and show non gamers (old people, young people, lame people)

RATING: 9
I really enjoy Takenoko because the board expands up as well as outwards, it involves setting up scenarios to collect points, it has a variety of actions one can take each turn, the theme is fun with cute miniatures and cards, and it involves a little luck, but not all luck.

My friends don't like this game because they think it has too much luck, but I enjoy being dealt a "hand" (In this game, it would be your hand of objectives and your dice rolls on your turns) and finding the best combos possible from what comes up. I could see how this game would be frustrating if you had a series of bad dice rolls (like in Machi Koro if you've ever played that), but I think the second phase of the turn is enough to mitigate that for me.

RATING: 6
nova verzija ima drugačne oznake na karticah
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"Takenoko" was a gift, and while we had seen it around, we never really looked too deeply at it. So far just Matt and I have played so we still need to see how it works with 3-4 players, but it feels like it would be just as fun.

Here goes our rating (1= disappointed, 3=hit&miss, 5= owned it!)

1. Originality - 4 - Concept wise I have never seen a storyline like it, not that I've seen every game ever, but it's definitely new in our collection. It does have a little Catan feel to it, but in a good way. I believe that the Catan feel will make it much easier to introduce to new and emerging gamers; and unlike Catan, "Takenoko" is meant to play well with just 2 players.

2. Artwork - 4 - It's cute, and the comic strip in the instructions really sets the tone for the game so everything feels like it fits together. 

3. Quality of Contents - 4 - In the box you will find that everything has it's place, but there's also some wriggle room if you would rather keep some tokens in the baggy. There's enough baggies for everything in the game, we just added elastics for the cards. The Gardner and the Panda are beautifully designed and don't feel flimsy, then again we don't have children so who knows what the lifespan of these figures will really be. Cards curve super easily and were curved fresh out of the box. Since we tend to do the elastic method instead of card sleeves - yes, we're horrible people - I'm not too concerned about this as our best-loved games tend to have the same issue.

4. Storyline - 5 - Love it. The comic strip sets up the story, giving you some background as to why this panda is roaming around with a gardner in bamboo fields, and it really adds personality to the pieces as you decide to move either figure.

5. Playthrough - 5 - Super easy and intuitive. In fact, it flows so well that we tend to forget to place our markers on the actions we took because we just moved through them so fluidly and are snatching the weather die from one another. We get serious about our bamboo!

6. Instructions - 4 - I learned the game from only using the instruction booklet. Everything fell into place once we got things set up and moving around. The only thing is that there are a few extra tiles that are not mentioned and our first run through we were concerned about how they might factor in. Pretty sure that they don't, it was just for even printing.

7. Replayability - 5- For a quick, simple, and mildly competitive game, I don't see why we wouldn't keep this game as a regular. It's not as intense as "King of Tokyo" by any means, but feels more social than "Carcassonne". 
	
8. Two player - 5- We've only played it two player so far and it works great.

9. Large Group N/A - max number is 4, but it feels like it would be just as smooth a game as two player.

10. Fun Factor - 4 - Overall I really do like the game, but for me personally, it doesn't get me jumping into "again, again!" mode. One game of "Takenoko" is all I need for one night. I do recommend it and will be introducing the game to friends, it's just not sitting on my "all-time favourite" shelf quite yet.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
A truly beautiful game about the emperor's bamboo gardener frustrated by the grazing of the emperor's new pet Panda.

Absolutely fantastic components give Takenoko great visual appeal and draws players into the game's theme.

There is basically no player contention in Takenoko, one need only focus on their current turn and not each others. The feel of playing Takenoko is relaxing, peaceful, laid-back: Zen.

Some of the objective cards aren't balanced at all. Oh, and be sure to play with the Advanced Variant rules.

After several plays I've come to realize that, despite its gorgeous appearance, there just isn't enough game to Takenoko. Its look is all the appeal. The lack of player interaction and lack of strategy are the problem. Players are performing actions, but there isn't any fun. If Matagot, with their cool components, hadn't made it, but Rio Grande had, with their bare bones boring tiles, no one would care about Takenoko.

Takenoko is okay. Good for kids, but adults will start selling/trading it after a year when the sparkle wears off. Glad I didn't shell out the cash for the overpriced Collector's Edition.

[b]Takenoko[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]This has been another hit with everyone in my house. It is also a great gateway game; my dad enjoys playing this with my kids which is awesome. I have to say it again, this is one of the most beautiful games we own, the board looks amazing by the end. [COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]- This is by far the most beautiful game we own; I love looking at the board as we play with all the bamboo growing.
- The panda and farmer are really well painted minis.
- The game oozes charm; people will be drawn in because of the great components.
- Easy to learn rules (we do not play with any variants).

I believe this is an excellent family/gateway game with easy to pick up rules. Takenoko oozes charm; people will be drawn in because of the great components. This game does have some luck involved, but that is great for a family game that you are going to play with younger kids and more casual gamers.[/i]

Plays 20+ have included the Chibis expansion.

規則:

*遊戲輪流進行，先擲天氣骰，在做動作(第一輪不擲)
*天氣骰 
太陽 多一個動作
雨天 已灌溉區域長一截竹子
風 兩個動作可相同
閃電 移動熊貓吃竹子
雲天 選擇一個設施拿
問號 自己選擇一個天氣
*做動作為五選二 不能重複
拿地景 抽三選一個放，另外兩個蓋著放回上面，可放位置 要連結中間或是連接任意兩塊，第一次有灌溉會長一截竹子
拿渠道 放渠道不算動作，但是放的不能改，必須從中間引水，第一次灌溉也會長
園丁 移動園丁直線停任意格，聽的該格跟相鄰同顏色如果有灌溉都長一截，最高四截
熊貓 直線移動，吃停下該格的竹子一截，保留來完成目標卡
抽目標卡 三種選一抽 完成的直接放在前面，未完成的隱藏手牌上限五張
*地形目標卡 一定要灌溉 熊貓目標卡 則要還對應竹子
*放置設施必須在沒有設施且沒有竹子的地形板塊上
*進階規則 抽到已完成的地形任務卡棄掉重抽
*玩家完成特定張數(2-3-4p:9-8-7張)目標卡拿到領主卡(+2)，其他玩家獲得最後一回合
*平手 熊貓目標卡分數高的獲勝

RATING: 7.2
熊貓竹園
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Beautiful components and artwork complements an elegant game that's both gamer and family accessible. You start with one of each of the three types of quest cards, which each have a condition you're trying to satisfy through the game to score points, be it eat 2 bamboo of a certain colour, grow a bamboo plant in a certain place or lay garden tiles in the configuration shown. Turns are a roll of the die which will generally earn you a bonus action, then take two actions - which you use to progress towards completing your quest cards if it looks like other people aren't going to accidentally fulfill the quest condition for you. As your usual actions are to lay a tile, grow a bamboo, or eat a bamboo, turns are very quick. There's continual satisfaction through the game as you tick quests off, there's interest in other player's turns in seeing if anyone might be progressing things in such a way as might help you, allowing you to work on other stuff Transamerica style. The game ends in the right timeframe and because turns are quick, and the game is easy to teach, it's non-gamer friendly - the beautiful bits help here as well. You can easily see yourself playing this a lot as a chilled out meaty semi-filler closer/opener type game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
When I saw the artwork I put Takenoko in my “Want to play” list.

When I read the rulebook I immediately promoted Takenoko to my wishlist and if I hadn’t bought so many board games last month, I would have surely ordered it.

Then I played it οn board game arena. I removed it from every list. 

I found it to be completely unbalanced. During the game I felt that I didn’t have control on anything at all. I didn’t feel that there was an objective to fulfill. I got completely bored making moves hopping for the game to end.

I was sure I was going to like it. I found the rules very interesting. I don’t know what is wrong with it but it just doesn’t work. Shame.-

P.S. Felt like the “Chasing the dragon” video game on South Park.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Takenoko is a contract fulfillment game in which players draw contracts randomly from one of three decks.  Basically, you're either trying to get the shared landscape to look a certain way, or have the panda consume a certain combination of bamboo colors.  The value of the contracts weren't the same, and oddly, did not often reflect its difficulty.  This proved particularly problematic as, after you hit your limit, players could not draw new contract cards until they had completed the ones they had.  So, the game really hinged on luck of the draw with contract cards.  On the other hand, the components are really outstanding.

(2 plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Light game where you manage the bamboo garden at the Imperial Japanese Court, including the emperors gift panda. The most striking thing about this game are the looks. I was very pleasantly surprised by the feeling that the garden expanded and the bamboo grew and was eaten as the gardener and the panda moved around. The pieces look very nice, including the prepainted gardener and panda miniatures. The only thing worth noting is that the bamboo pieces doesn't always fit into each other.

Short overview of play:

Each player starts with three scoring cards, one of each kind (tiles,gardener and panda)

Each turn, the player chooses two of five different actions. Place a tile, take an irrigation piece, move the gardener, move the panda and take a scoring card. The player may also score one or more cards during his turn.

The tile action allows the player to take three tiles, choose one and place it in the garden. If a pattern of irrigated tiles matching a scoring card is created, that card can be scored.

The irrigation action allows the player to take an irrigation piece. The acquired piece can be placed in the garden immediately or saved on the player mat for later use. Placing irrigation pieces is not an action in itself, so can be done immediately following a tile action.

The gardener moves around the garden, making bamboo grow. If there are bamboo growth matching the pattern on a scoring card, that card can be scored.

The panda moves around the garden, eating the growing bamboo which the player collects on his player mat. If the bamboo pieces on the mat matches the set on a scoring card, those pieces can be discarded back to the box to score that card.

Taking a scoring card does just that.

Play continues until one player has scored a number of cards determined by the number of players. Then all other players take an extra turn and the scoring cards for each player are summed up to determine the winner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5

Takenoko is a game where you are manipulating and building a bamboo forest, while simultaneously fending/feeding off the Emperor's panda. The entire game revolves around playing scoring cards to earn VP, and these cards also are the timer for the endgame. As such, if you don't manage your ratios of acquiring cards, completing the things you need  to play cards, and then playing your cards (and hypothetically, keeping your enemies from doing so). The fact that you're getting unintentionally wrecked the whole time by the other players doesn't help.

This, plus the fact that you gain some bonus randomly based on a die-roll, means you don't have the sort of control that I want. 

Overall there's too much randomness in a game that on first glance seems it should be a euro, and that irritates me.

RATING: 5
Found this to be merely alright--too light for my tastes strategically. I'm not convinced there is great balance between the scoring cards, and some house rules seem necessary to make up for the fact you can draw some that are already or nearly complete compared to some that require a lot of work. The die to determine what extra action you get can also be quite annoying and allow certain players to get moves they want while blocking others. Overall it feels like there is a really interesting game that could be had here, but with the rules as they are this is simply much too random for me to really want to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Takenoko is a prototypical example of what I don't like about contemporary middle-weight Euros:

* Limited, indirect, and ultimately uninteresting player interaction. Here, players help each other indirectly (players build and improve the board/garden together, and players score for certain completed configurations). However, because objectives are secret, players don't really know how they are helping each other, and it's a waste of time to try to deduce your opponents' objectives.

* Hidden, randomly determined personal objectives, which dramatically reduces strategic depth and player interaction -- players are not going to bother to deduce what objectives their opponents' have, nor do they have the time to hinder them

* Bloated with too many rules and mechanisms considering the game's strategic and tactical shallowness

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Component Quality:
Excellent. Thick tiles and beautiful painted miniatures make this a very nice game to look at. The box insert stores everything perfectly (but doesn't leave room for sleeving the cards)

Gameplay:
Simple. After one reading of the short rulebook I had all the actions down. It took a single game to memorize the different weather effects too. I would recommend players start with the advanced rules right away to help eliminate a little bit of luck. Takenoko is very light on strategy and really comes down to drawing the right cards at the right time (which is almost completely random). It plays a lot like Ticket to Ride, but with less strict goals (no negative points for failing to complete a goal). The theme helps make it fun and turns flow smoothly, but if you're wanting anything besides a cake-walk out of Takenoko, I recommend using some house-rules. A variant or two could make this a real gem.

RATING: 8.47
Our first play of this was 4 new players....and it felt long, slow, and not overly engaging.  Then, we played several more times as a 2P game where it really shines.  The game goes quickly.  25min game time.  You have to set proper expectations...don't expect too much, but it's a fun experience.

Here's what I'd recommend:
1) when you achieve an objective, play it, don't hold it in your hand.  It's nice to see who's winning.  This helps with some good banter around the table.
2) don't use the 'advanced rule.'  That rule makes the plot cards less desirable late in the game.  It's a great thing to pick up an objective card that is already complete.....lay down the card and score the points.

Link to video review:  http://www.boardgamegeek.com/video/40068/takenoko/dice-tower-reviews-takenoko-the-charters-play-th

RATING: 6
Can a game be awesome and yet very bland and boring? Takenoko seems to be that game... it undeniably scores very high in the components and aesthetics department, the main appeal to what is an otherwise flat game.

That's not to say it's bad however, Bauza is completely on target with the audience and the game is interesting enough, but the luck mitigation and chaos control is low, ultimately making it a non-starter for gamers wanting a bit more than a cute game to play with the kids.

So... possibly one of the best family game out there, but fails to really shake the genre in any way, there's certainly no Dixit to be found here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a very nice looking and fun little game. 

My only real problem with this game is the balance of the objective cards in games with more than two players. In the game, there are three different colors of tiles with different distributions- there are more green tiles and fewer pink tiles. Thus, the objectives for the pink tiles are worth more points to accomplish than the yellow, which in turn are worth more points than the green objectives. In theory this makes sense, and I think they are pretty balanced for the 2 player game. However, once you get multiple people in the game, the balance goes out the door. In those games, you can have multiple people working on the same color and that color becomes much easier to get on the board than the value of the objectives would indicate. 

For an example: in one game I played, three of the players drew multiple pink objectives at the start of the game and the fourth drew all green objectives. The three "pink" players all dug through the stack of tiles looking for pink tiles, irrigating the pink tiles and growing the pink bamboo. The "green" player was working solo growing up the green tiles. By the time the "green" player finished his first objective, all three "pink" players had finished at least two pink objectives. The pink objectives were much easier to get, and they were worth a lot more points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Two player game is slightly flawed. The bamboo collection objectives are the easiest to complete, so there is little incentive to use the other two objective types. Approaching the the game in this way also makes it less necessary to expand titles, purchase irrigation channels, or use improvements. Using the advanced player rules varient (prohibiting a player from taking an objective card already complete in the garden) further disincentivizes playing gardener and plot objective cards. 

A four player game would require players to vary their strateges. However, adding players increases the downtime between turns.

The game is a bit repetitive, playing the same few actions turn after turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I have been intrigued by the many pictures of this game on BGG. July 2014, I got to try it out at the opening event of FLGS [url=http://demospel.be/]Demo-Spel[/url] and I immediately bought a copy (the fact they gave 10% off on that day did, of course, help me with that decision).

The components for this game are absolutely gorgeous. Prepainted miniatures for the gardener and panda, sturdy cardboard plot tiles for the garden, wooden irrigation channels and pieces of bamboo. All very colourful and family-friendly.

Goal of the game is to get the most points by completing objective cards. Depending on the number of players, the game ends with a final turn after one player completes a set amount of objectives. There's three kinds of objectives: Panda - where points are scored by making the panda eat certain colors of bamboo, Plot - where points are scored by placing the tiles in certain colors and shapes on the table, and Bamboo - where points are scored by growing bamboo to certain hights/on certain tiles/in certain amounts.

To achieve their objectives, the players get two actions per turn - though they can't take the same action twice. They can move the panda and eat bamboo, move the gardener to grow bamboo, place additional plots of land on the table, irrigate said plots, or add another objective card to their hand (max. five). After the first turn, the players also roll a die, to add a random event to their turn (grow bamboo of a certain color, get a third action, be allowed to take the same action twice,...)

Turns flow fast and the game is relatively conflict-free. Players are mostly focusing on achieving their own objectives, instead of working agaist their fellow players - though this is possible, depending on the playstyle of your group. This flexible stance against player conflict, makes this suitable for many different kinds of groups.

[b][u]Painted the miniatures?:[/u][/b] No. They are two of the most beautiful pre-painted pawns I have ever seen.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I wasn't sure about the strategy of this one until someone said something that really clicked. It's kinda like rock-paper-scissors: if people are placing tiles for Emperor cards, you should use their land to grow bamboo for the gardener; if they are growing for gardener cards, you should eat their bamboo with the panda; if they are eating for panda cards, you should place tiles, I guess.

In theory if you can read what the other players are doing you can capitalise and do better. In practise I draw a lot of green bamboo cards and lose to someone who drew a lot of pink. My first few games of this devolved into everyone placing tiles and picking up Emperor cards, and whoever blind-drew the most already completed cards won. Imagine my surprise when I heard people saying that Emperor is the weakest strategy! I definitely need to play more of this to implement what I wrote above. And maybe house-rule a way to exchange unwanted (green) cards for better (pink) ones. I have Chibis now too.

RATING: 7
Cute theme. Beautiful components. The give and take of the gardener and the panda provide a nice balance for each other. Playing a game of Takenoko always feels very relaxing, which is not something I usually look for (I love high-stress, high tension games) but given the game's theme, the zen feel really seems to work.

One problem I have with Takenoko is that it often feels like multiplayer solitaire. Everyone is using the same board, so there are ways to block others from getting points - placing a tile somewhere inconvenient or eating bamboo from a tile someone has been gardening - but none of these are very direct, and they are stalls more than anything else. More player interaction would improve this game.

RATING: 7.75
We love the adorable Kawai inspired artwork and the easy to comprehend, yet strategic decisions of Takenoko. Each game plays quickly yet is completely different owing to the myriad of ways the tiles get placed. We like that the game has some cooperative elements with players often assisting others to build irrigation channels out to dry bamboo tiles, yet each player is secretly scheming precisely how they alone will benefit most from a certain configuration of bamboo. There is an element of luck with the weather die, but it can often be mitigated by judicious selection of your subsequent moves. The component quality is generally good although the cardstock for the player mats is very flimsy and each person should get three wooden turn markers instead of two for instances when you roll the sun. Other than those minor or quibbles, the components are top notch especially the painted panda and farmer figures.

Nov 2017 update: Our Collector's edition Takenoko arrived today and we broke it right out to play after Turkey Day leftovers. We love this game so much! This extra large size version is even better. Exquisite components and art and an exceptional case. Best of all we won it on auction for $100!

RATING: 1.5
Better than Tokaido. Not sure if that's saying much. Certainly I see a design approach: let's put scores on cards and cards tell you what you do. Seems a bit lazy: here there actions you can do, and here are cards which reward these actions. I don't see anything that would tie the game together, on the mechanical level at least. It's just pieces put together in the same box. 

While the game is an enjoyable family type fare, there's really no rhyme or reason to it's theme. Who are we? What are we doing? What's this panda doing in the garden? The booklet explains it, with this far fetched tale of [i]"you're master zen gardeners and emperor sent you a panda which is eating up the garden". [/i] Uhm? Ok. It gets better: "you must impress the emperor". What? That idiot who sent us this menace of a panda in the first place? 

Verdict: it's the panda game. If you need a panda in a family game, get this, otherwise it's nothing else that makes it stand out of the pack. I also begin to see Hanabi as that weird inspired game in Bauza's otherwise pretty samey ouvre.

RATING: 7
Takenoko is a chaotic game of bamboo cultivation, which is probably one of the cutest themes I've ever heard of. The game is family-friendly - it has high production value and the rules are simple enough so that you can just pick up and play, although the rules for irrigation always escape me for some reason.

This game, however, is best played with 2 players. The hidden objectives may be fun as players curse their opponents for unwittingly disrupting their plans, but a lot of control over what happens on the board is lost with more players and some objectives become fiendishly difficult to achieve. Just keep in mind that this is a light, tactical game and luck plays a significant role in it.

RATING: 6
The cute panda miniature, wooden bamboo pieces, and colorful tiles are attractive and make this a good game to play with non-gamer friends and family.  Oh, the comic in on the front of the rule book that tells the back story is funny too.  Other than that, the game itself is just okay.  Players try to accomplish three different types of goals (expanding the zoo, growing bamboo, or feeding the panda) by placing tiles and improvement tiles or moving the gardener or panda.  There's a few little other things going on, but that's basically the game.  It works and it's cute, but it's not great.  It's also a race game, so the first player to hit a certain number of completed cards triggers the last round.  However, that player first gets a couple bonus points and then the other players get one more turn to try to catch up. That's never really possible and so it feels like the end game isn't quite right.  This game feels like a re-worked and re-themed Ticket to Ride in many ways.  Overall, it's just okay, but my wife will occasionally play this game with me and that's good, especially since she doesn't like to play games very often.

I am dumbfounded as to why they decided to produce a large collector's version of this game nearly 2 years after it was originally released.  It's just an okay game that was never even very popular.  I don't understand, but I guess if the goal was to get people talking about the game again, then the definitely accomplished that...

Amazon had a goldbox deal on boardgames, and I went with this instead of Pandemic. 

As many people have pointed out, I don't think the objectives are balanced. IMO panda cards yield slightly too many points, and plot cards not enough. Green plot scoring cards are pretty much useless, IMO, and I am always reluctant to draw from that deck for fear of pulling one. It wouldn't be so bad late in the game, but it's easiest to complete the plot objectives early (we play the "advanced" variant). Having that card load your hand for the whole game is annoying. 

On the other hand, drawing 2 of the 3 green/yellow/red panda cards gives you a slight advantage. Six points feels like too much for that card. Probably, (again, just my opinion) panda cards should be worth 1 pt less, and most plot cards should be worth 1 pt more. Could just be the way we play, though, since we don't have all that many plays.

A gyönyörű pandás, bambuszos játékot már régóta ki szerettem volna próbálni, sajnos csalódást okozott. Bár már felkészültem a csalódásra, mert olvastam rossz híreket a játékról. Egy bambuszkeretet menedzselnek a játékosok, ahol két-két akciója, plusz egy szerencsefaktor szerinti kockadobás plusz akciója is van a játékosoknak köreikben. Lehet hatszögletű lapkákkal a területet bővíteni, öntözőcsatornát építeni ezekhez. Lehet kertésszel bambuszt nevelni, illetve pandával megdézsmálni. Háromfajta célkártya szerezhető a játékban folyamatosan, amiből minél többet próbálunk megoldani. Sajnos ez a játék gyenge pontja. Mintha nem gondolták volna végig a célkártyák nehézségeit. Teljesen logikátlan némely kártya szerezhető pontszáma. A kártyafajták nehézségei is nagyon különbözőek. Az pedig teljesen a szerencsén múlik kihez milyen kártya jut. Jobb lett volna 3-at húz 1-et választ megoldással ezt kipróbálniuk a készítőknek, mert ez így nem lett túl jó.
Könnyed, családi játék a Takenoko, amely legnagyobb érdem a kivitele, a festett figurákkal és gyönyörű bambuszokkal. Sajnos nem nyűgözött le a mechanizmus terén.

RATING: 6.5
1 play, 4er

This is a cute, pretty light, family style game with pretty bits, best played quickly. It's got a very nicely sculpted panda and farmer figures, cool little bamboo stem sculpts in 3 colors, and nicely illustrated terrain tiles. I'd be happy to play it, but I prefer heavier games.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Place a water tile in the center, with farmer and panda tokens on it, and 6 terrain tiles around it. Deal each player 1 scoring card from each of the 3 decks.

On your turn, you get 2 different actions plus a random (d6) bonus action. The bonus may be:
- an extra action
- take the same action twice
- grow a bamboo somewhere already on the board
- take a terrain modifier token (limited supply; these ban panda from hex, make bamboo grow faster, or irrigate hex even without irrigation stick)
- move panda anywhere
- wild; choose one of above
The normal actions allow you to:
- add terrain tile (draw 3, keep 1)
- place irrigation stick, extending from central hex or another piece; any hex newly irrigated gets a free bamboo stem of that terrain color
- move farmer in straight line: bamboos on final tile and adjacent tiles of same color grow
- move panda in straight line: eat a stem of bamboo on tile you land upon
- draw a scoring card (from 3 decks, corresponding to terrain configurations, bamboo colors eaten, or bamboos colors/sizes growing on the board).
You can play the scoring cards face up during your turn when you satisfy their conditions.

The game ends when any player has 7 face up cards (everyone else gets one final turn); most VP wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I play enough board games that I can recognize the value in a game like this one as a family game.  The game is cute as hell with bright whimsical colors and cute painted farmer and panda figurines and stackable pastel-colored bamboo.  It is very simple to teach and understand and plays in 45 minutes or less.  And the theme itself is very family friendly, just a farmer growing colorful bamboo for a chubby panda to chow down on.

Even with all that recognition though I just get so little value out of a game like this.  I don't have kids and I rarely ever play with kids.  As such my gaming tastes usually include much more depth and much more interesting decisions.  This game is about split in two with half of it being complete luck and the other half being tactical decision making but usually the best decision is incredibly obvious.

The game is played with players taking actions to control the makeup of a board and the pieces on it.  The actions allow you to place new board tiles, irrigate tiles so bamboo will grow there, moving a farmer around to grow bamboo, moving a panda around to eat bamboo, and finally, drawing objective cards.

The objective cards are the win condition of the game, they give you points based on placing certain board tiles, growing certain colors of bamboo, and eating certain colors of bamboo with the panda.  The reason this game is 50% luck is you have no control over the objectives you draw, very little control over the tiles you draw, and a random event occurs at the beginning of each of your turns.  Some times you can draw an objective card that is almost (or completely) impossible to achieve, so drawing it is a completely wasted action.

The other half, as I said, involves tactical use of your available actions to achieve these goals but as I said, the best option is usually very apparent.  If you have 2/3 of a board layout objective done your action is to draw new tiles.  If you need to grow a 4-high stalk of pink bamboo and the farmer is in line with a 3-high stalk then you take the farmer action.  If he's not in line with it then you don't take that action and hope your opponent moves him into a better position later.

It's very paint-by-numbers with very little chance to come ahead via game play.  9 times out of 10 the winner of this game just drew more easily achievable or valuable objectives and I stand by that estimate.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.4
Great elegant designed game with great depth for such a "light" game.  Can be played really quickly 
(30 mins) with some light meaningful decisions to be made. 

Has some luck involved which I like a bit of to keep things fresh and not overly "static".

I never got Ticket to Ride, it was too simple for me. Very nice game and elegant but I just didn't prefer playing it.

Takenoko replaces Ticket to Ride for me as it's still a light gateway game that can be taught quickly to non-gamers and played quickly, yet is a slight step up in terms of complexity. Takenoko is a slightly deeper set collecting (with 3 ways to do so) game than Ticket to Ride and for such a light game I like that it has a sort of personal worker placement board mechanic where you choose which two actions from a list that you'd like to do for each turn as well as a separate differnt list of actions that you can perform that is determined by a dice roll. Ingenius really - random and non-random all in one for some balance.

This is still only a filler for me.  I much prefer to play a game that is deeper than Takenoko but for such a light game and this niche level it's really a great game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players work on the same Imperial garden and score points for completing hidden objectives: get the panda to eat a certain combination of coloured bamboo; grow bamboo in a certain place to a certain height; place tiles in a certain configuration.

Really stunning production, including little gardener and panda figures and stackable wooden bamboo shoots, but the game itself is light and luck-heavy.

I'm rather worried about perverse incentives, because it seems to me that if other players are pro-actively attempting to fulfil their objectives, the ideal strategy is destructive leeching: do nothing but eat bamboo and draw panda-objective cards, which hurts other players at the same time as it helps you. But of course it's a Prisoner's Dilemma; if everyone is destructive the strategy works for no-one.

It's OK as a light game, particularly for non-gamers, but I don't think it holds up to repeated play.

RATING: 7
A light, beautiful game of fulfilling personal goals. The turns are extremely quick and can be somewhat repetitive, but the goal cards are what keep the game interesting so you don't really notice the limitations of your actions. The game shows its depth in subsequent plays when you learn you're not only trying to fulfill your goals before the other players, but you're also striving to be economical in your movement and your choices of which goals to fulfill. The theme of Takenoko is somewhat charming, focused around a panda bear and a farmer, and could probably be enjoyed by all types of gamers. It can feel a little "meh" at times, but I find myself asking for it more often than I thought I would.

RATING: 6.5
Rated a 7 after my first play. It works nicely for a group of non-gamers to get into the gaming world. I'd consider it a fun little gateway. I'm not sure it has anything special about it besides it's welcoming artwork and loveable theme, but it's a nice game none the less.

The first time I played it, Enna won (9 years old), so that says something about the random factor. Anybody can win based on the card draws and how things play out.

I'm sure there are a few blocking strategies, but after the first play, it really felt luck driven in what objective cards you draw.

Beautiful presentation, though. I'll keep it for now, but I'm not sure it will stay in my collection for the long haul.

::UPDATE::

Takenoko is very much hyped for delivering a light-hearted game, with beautiful/cute/attractive/fun components. This assessment is correct.

If someone is interested in a game that delivers the above, then Takenoko will not disappoint. However...if someone wants a game that is filled with great gameplay, including strategy and planning, this is not your game.

I'll pull it out every now and then to entertain those who want a simple game with a simple rule set, with simply beautiful pieces. I won't pull it out when I'm with anybody who wants a real strategic gaming experience.

RATING: 6.5

Takenoko is a light family game with a decent amount of randomness. You're laying tiles, growing bamboo, setting loose a voracious panda, and trying to accomplish goals on your scoring cards. Every turn you roll a die and get a special ability. Then you either draw a scoring card, move the gardener (grow stuff), move the panda (eat stuff), draw three land tiles and place one, or get a irrigation token. The game goes until someone scores seven scoring cards... that person gets a 2 point bonus for ending the game and everyone else gets another turn.

The game is largely about getting the right scoring cards (you have a hand limit of five) and accomplishing them. This is broken into 3 flavors, land cards where you score if certain color configurations of irrigated tiles appear on the board, panda cards which score if you return the correct color/quantity of bamboo you've eaten, and gardener cards where you score if the correct amount of bamboo is on the board on the correct tiles.

The combination of random card draws where some cards are simply easier to accomplish, although they reward you less, and the random die roll every turn probably keep this game from being something I want to play too often. On my first play, everyone spent the last turn drawing random scoring cards in the hope of getting something they could already accomplish. I won, but only because the second place player drew two scoring cards as his turn and got the card he could have accomplished last, when he no longer had an action left to make it good.

It's a short enough game that the randomness isn't too damning... but this is probably a game that I'd only really play as a family game or filler. With decent players the winner is going to be whoever lucked out on card draws.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
May 2016 Guardtower
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/70919/takenoko]Takenoko[/url] is one of the prettiest games of 2012, but isn't in the contest for the best one. Maybe this happened because I played only once in a 2 player game.

The game asks you to attend the needs of a Panda, and also make the garden bigger and the bambu taller. The gameplay revolves around objectives that reward points when completed, and these objectives are: make the garden in a given pattern (using the tiles), grow bambus in a certain way or to feed the Panda some sort of bambu. I'm under the impression that the types of objectives aren't really balanced - the ones that require the bambus to be in some way were much easier to complete and award more points than the ones that require to feed the Panda, that I found much more difficult to do.

Anyway, [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/70919/takenoko]Takenoko[/url] plays fast (our game took around 40 minutes to end, with explanations), catches the eye (it will surely be a hit in conventions) and it is proper for family game nights (the confrontation factor is low).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A long time ago at the Japanese Imperial court, the Chinese Emperor offered a giant panda bear as a symbol of peace to the Japanese Emperor. Since then, the Japanese Emperor has entrusted his court members (the players) with the difficult task of caring for the animal by tending to his bamboo garden.

In Takenoko, the players will cultivate land plots, irrigate them, and grow one of the three species of bamboo (Green, Yellow and Pink) with the help of the Imperial gardener to maintain this bamboo garden. They will have to bear with the immoderate hunger of this sacred animal for the juicy and tender bamboo. The player who manages his land plots best, growing the most bamboo while feeding the delicate appetite of the panda, will win the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This is one of those games where you look at the high quality, finely executed and very cute game pieces and think "this is for kids", or at best this will be an excruciatingly light game. The comic book documentation reinforces this perception.  Once the game starts what you get is an experience that is simple enough to grasp yet takes at least 2-3 turn preplanning to play well.  Players draw random cards that act like missions and roll a die that provides a random bonus action.  However, the players, rather than chance, drive the game because the interaction of rules and components allow for planning and flexible choices to meet missions.  For example, players create the game board by placing tiles, drawing three and placing one.  The game works well and looks even better.  Play it or be a sad Panda...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
with house rules to fix the problem of the panda card strategie
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I very much wanted to like this game. Everything about this game screamed "mid-heavy Euro with amazing components". Alas, while the last part of the statement is true, the initial statement is not. While the rules feel as if the game is Euro-centric, the game somehow feels quite chaotic. The illusion of choice comes from all the actions and the weather die roll. However, I felt that all the decisions in this game were pre-programmed and from the get-go, I knew what I had to do given my bonus cards at hand. There is little doubt about the outcome of my actions except that they are solely governed by the cards at hand. I feel the lack of tension and also the lack of serious decision making.
 With two, it seems manageable. However, I would hazard a guess that with more players, the game becomes a whole lot less predictable and harder to achieve the individual goals. 
  A huge warning about this game: the variant rule MUST be used in this game regarding scoring of newly drawn cards that can score immediately upon selection without any effort. This is a game breaker here. I don't understand why the rules aren't designed to include this rule. It is ridiculous that one can score multiple cards with existing tiles/bamboo configurations without any effort whatsoever.

05/2014: After a few more plays with slightly modified rules for card draw, we are satisfied that the rules balance out each other. 
1) We can have no more than TWO similar types of objective cards per hand with a maximum total limit of 5 per hand as per the usual rule.
2) Each set of three objective cards score an additional 3 points.

RATING: 5
This game has probably suffered low ratings from some users due to the results of horribly written instructions that are literally missing huge key pieces of information that affect how strategic the game is.  Took a couple of plays to realize that there was a reason why some of the gardener cards were so valuable and some of the pattern cards much less so.  Took the game from a 5 to a 7 just figuring this out - and this information isn't even in the rules!  Not the deepest game, but fun and beautiful.

Update: That said, I just don't feel the urge to play this game that often.  While I love the thematic mechanics - Panda moves and eats, gardener moves and bamboo grows - and the sheer beauty of the game, there just isn't enough there to grab me.  I also don't think it succeeds as a gateway in the sense that it's highly replayable.  Ticket to Ride is simpler, but it's a game that beginners will want to play over and over.  Just don't see it in Takenoko.

So with that I drop my rating to a 6 and I figure it will stay there.

Update: Have played Tokaido twice so far and it's the game I wished Takenoko had been.  Yes, Tokaido is light, but at 2p it offers both a more zen-like experience AND a feeling of both theme progression (literally - you're traveling Japan) and tactical choices.  Tokaido has some critics who claim there are no choices, but that is false, at least at 2p.  Takenoko just felt so repetitive - a feeling that stripped away the beauty and otherworld zen experience of the colors and theme.  Because with both of these games, those are the major selling points.  Take that way and what you have left is so fluffy light that neither game would be worth playing.

RATING: 7.5

Theme really, really carries this one, though -- makes it easy to explain, and a game that everyone will want to really love. A true gateway/non-gamer/feel-good low competition game, definitely worthwhile to get if you have friends who aren't regular gamers.

RATING: 9
I avoided this game because of the hard to figure out name and the childish looking theme of a panda in some emperor's gardens.  That was a mistake.  The game has simple to learn rules with 5 possible turn actions.  The weather dice adds a nice twist and element to the game influencing what type of objectives you can finish most efficiently.  While it was easy to learn and start playing, continued play has added layers of complexity to decision making getting used to what objectives are left to be drawn (expert mode where completed objectives cannot be picked up but you keep drawing until you get one incomplete) and the likelihood of drawing one you can complete with remaining actions in the last few turns to influence what type you pick.  The depth of strategy in the game has made me a huge fan even though luck is always a factor and can dominate a single play of the game using careful tactical decision making will yield a higher than average winrate.

RATING: 4.5
When I first played this game, I was completely endeared with the adorable components and quick turns.  After purchasing the game and playing it a few more times, I found myself extremely bored and a little agitated while playing.  

The hidden goals that you're all trying to complete just take all of the gameness out of the game.  You may be screwing over other people, but you don't get the pleasure of knowing that you are.  Which makes it even more frustrating when someone messes up your plans because they don't even know they're doing it either.

I'm not one who cares much about winning, In fact I got this game specifically because it was a game not meant to be taken seriously.  I surprised myself how the end game always frustrated me as you can be SO close to winning, and just keep grabbing cards hoping for one you need to win and you can't get it.

RATING: 7
Neat little game! I love Antoine Bauza's designs, and especially Tokaido, so I came in to this game knowing that there was going to be a good game with not too much complexity, but enough interaction to keep it interesting, and I was correct. I think my favorite part about the game was the die roll at the beginning of the turn, because it means that you can plan your turn out to an extent but that die roll can give you so much more to work with. The game feels very reactionary, and to it's credit, the simplicity of the design keeps it interesting because there's always that unknown factor of what action you might have for this turn.

RATING: 6
I take this game everywhere I go to work, It is a easy game to explain and to play, however I have had mixed results with it. In general, Girls and kids love it because its cuteness and are willing to play; the first reaction of men is.. meh. It turns quite puzzly and the fun part is when it becomes a race to finish. However it is boring, it lacks this EUREKA feeling and player interaction; it becomes worst when everybody is following the easy path (panda cards) and not trying to keep in mind the scores of others and just doing one card after the other. I used to play it as trying to read other player's movements. it plays very well with any number of players and non gamer, though, but it is not for everybody. My girlfriend loves it, for me is the staple, my mother finds it very fiddly and does not have depth or interaction for my friends.
